# Excel: Financial Functions in Depth

> Master the art of financial analysis using Excel through this comprehensive course. Join instructor Danielle Stein Fairhurst to explore key concepts like calculating loan payments, principal and interest splits, and depreciation using various methods. Advanced modules cover forecasting investment values, determining rates of return, and building financial models using dynamic arrays and new Excel 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886) | 2h 29m | Intermediate | 48K learners

### 1. Analyzing Loans, Payments, and Interest in Excel

#### PMT: Calculate a loan payment

#### PPMT and IPMT: Calculate the principal and interest payments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The total loan repayment that we calculated using the PMT function includes the principal and the interest.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=8)** However, we often need to split the loan repayment into its separate components, and we often need to do this when we're creating financial statements.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=20)** So we would need to show the interest amount on the income statement and the principal amount on the balance sheet.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=30)** Again, Excel can help us do this with the IPMT and the PPMT functions.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=38)** The total repayment will always be the same, but the amount of each of the interest and principal components is going to change over time.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=47)** The interest on a loan might look like this.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=51)** Over the first couple of years, the interest amount is going to be higher, but that will reduce as we progress throughout the term of the loan.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=60)** The balance will be the principal.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=62)** So in the first couple of years, a large proportion will be interest, and as we get towards the end of the loan term, it's almost all principal.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=72)** So we can see that the total payment is exactly the same throughout the entire life of the loan.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=79)** We're going to have a look at how to create a chart like this in the next movie, but first let's have a look at how to calculate interest and principal using the IPMT and PPMT functions in Excel.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=94)** So here we've got it set up.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=96)** This is very similar to the PMT function that we did earlier.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=101)** The only difference here is that, is that we need to know the current period.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=107)** We didn't need that with the PMT function because all of the payments were exactly the same.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=113)** So here we're going to add the PPMT function.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=120)** Again, the rate, we are doing this as a monthly repayment, so we're going to divide it by 12.
>

> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/ppmt-and-ipmt-calculate-the-principal-and-interest-payments?u=76281980&t=137)** The number of periods is 25 years.

#### EFFECT and NOMINAL: Nominal and effective interest rates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Most of the time, banks will advertise returns to investors using a nominal interest rate or the APR, the annual percentage rate.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=11)** But sometimes it's expressed as an effective interest rate, the APY or annual percentage yield.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=19)** You need to be able to convert one to the other, and Excel can help us to do that.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=26)** But first, let's have a look at the difference between the nominal and effective rates.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=32)** So the nominal interest rate is the stated interest rate of the bond or the loan, which is the actual rate that the borrower will pay.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=40)** So this is often referred to as the coupon rate.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=43)** So for example, if the nominal rate on a loan is say 5%, borrowers can expect to pay $5 of interest for every $100 loaned to them every year for the life of the loan.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=58)** The effective rate, on the other hand, factors in the impact of compounding interest.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=65)** So you not only pay the $5 in interest, but you'd also pay interest on the interest accumulated in prior periods.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=74)** Now this is not to be confused with the real interest rate, which factors inflation into the equation.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=81)** So if you're comparing two investment opportunities, you need to be able to make sure that you are comparing the same type of interest rate.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=91)** Let's have a look now at how to convert nominal to effective and effective to nominal using Excel.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=100)** So we are going to use the nominal function here because we've been given the effective interest rate of 6%, and we know that there are 12 periods in the year.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=112)** So we're just going to go in and type nominal, open bracket, the effective rate is 6%, and the number of periods is 12.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=124)** So that will convert it to a nominal rate of 5.84%.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=132)** Similarly, if we were given the nominal rate of say 6%, and again with 12 periods in the year, we could use the effect function to convert the 6%, 12 periods into an effective interest rate of 6.17%.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=154)** So taking into account the effect of compounding interest, the effective interest rate is always going to be slightly higher, and we can see how each of these functions interact with each other.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=167)** So if we were to say a nominal interest rate of 5.84%, we'll convert it back to the 6% that we were given.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=176)** Or similarly, if we were to take an effective rate of 6.17%, that will also convert it back to 6%.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/effect-and-nominal-nominal-and-effective-interest-rates?u=76281980&t=186)** So these different functions can help you to compare different investments using similar interest rates.

### 2. Calculating Depreciation with Excel Functions

#### DB: Calculate depreciation using the declining balance method
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As we've discussed, straight-line depreciation is exactly the same for each period over the life of the asset.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=9)** However, sometimes we need to accelerate that depreciation amount.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=14)** Instead of depreciating it evenly, we're going to take more depreciation at the beginning and reduce it as we go along.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=22)** This is called a declining balance depreciation method, and the DB function we're going to look at first calculates it on a fixed declining balance basis.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=33)** So we might do this if we expect to gain more revenue from the asset in the first couple of years, or it could be that if we take more depreciation at the beginning of an asset's life, though that money is worth more, as inflation eats away at the value of money, so dollars in later years would be worth less than values in earlier years.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=55)** If you take more depreciation at the beginning of an asset's life, then that money is worth more in effective dollars.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=64)** Let's take a look now at how to calculate fixed declining balance depreciation in Excel, and we're going to do that using the DB function.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/db-calculate-depreciation-using-the-declining-balance-method?u=76281980&t=74)** So we've got the initial cost of the asset, the salvage value, and our economic life is eight.
>

> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=174)** So that asset is going to cost you 69 1/2 thousand dollars, and it's going to save you this much money over the subsequent five years.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=185)** So your company has stated that its weighted average cost of capital is 6.2%.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=192)** So any opportunities or anything we evaluate financially, we need to do it at the weighted average cost of capital rate of 6.2%.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=204)** We'll talk a little later on about how to calculate the WACC in later chapters.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=212)** So, let's go ahead and have a look at this opportunity.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=216)** So what it's saying here is that if we were to make this investment and receive these cash flows in the future, what is the net present value of those cash flows?
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=228)** If I was to add this up, I can see that it's actually worth $3,171.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=237)** That is not what the net present value is going to give us because that $15,000 that we receive in five years' time is going to be discounted back by 6.2%, 6.2%, right back into the present day.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=253)** So, let's go ahead and use our NPV function based on the 6.2%, and that is giving us a negative NPV value.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=268)** So, even though the total was a positive value, the NPV is negative.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=274)** So that is basically telling us that this is not worth going ahead.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=278)** If something is giving you a negative NPV, that means that it is not worth going ahead, assuming, of course, that our weighted average cost of capital is 6.2%.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/npv-net-present-value-of-an-investment?u=76281980&t=291)** So, in that case, I would say not to go ahead with this opportunity.

#### XNPV: Net present value given irregular inputs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we are using the NPV function, we are a little bit limited in the way that we can enter our cash flow values.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=10)** Because of the way that the function is structured, each of the series of cash flows needs to be staged exactly 12 months apart from each other.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=22)** Cash flow doesn't always work like that, often, we know exactly when the funds are going to be paid or received, and they're not always exactly 12 months apart.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=34)** So this is where the XNPV function can be useful.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=39)** So if we know exactly the date that the funds are going to go out or come in, then we can use this function to calculate it.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=51)** So let's have a go at calculating it.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=56)** Again, the rate here, the values are there, and these are the dates.

>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=72)** Just bear in mind, though, that it is slightly different in the way that it is calculating, which can be a little bit confusing.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=81)** So what the NPV function does is it discounts everything back to the 1st of January or the very first date in the calculation, so that $75,000 is not being discounted at all.

> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=110)** So there's a little bit of a difference between the way these two functions are calculating.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/xnpv-net-present-value-given-irregular-inputs?u=76281980&t=117)** But depending on how your cash flows are structured, you might like to just stick with the NPV function, or you might like the additional functionality of the XNPV function.

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/irr-internal-rate-of-return?u=76281980&t=9)** And that is basically saying that if we were to pay out this much money in the first period and receive this much over the next couple of years, the annual return, what that is on average as an annual rate is the internal rate of return.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/irr-internal-rate-of-return?u=76281980&t=27)** And we can calculate this by using the IRR function.

>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/irr-internal-rate-of-return?u=76281980&t=67)** Most of the time, we want it to be closest to 10%.

#### Introduction to LAMBDA functions with BYCOL and BYROW
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've talked a bit in previous chapters about dynamic array ranges.

> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=143)** In the same way, we can do a BYROW at the top here.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=147)** So if you wanted to sum across, we could do a BYROW and we can hash, and then we could do exactly the same thing with a SUM.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=158)** The BYROW will work exactly the same as the BYCOL.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=163)** But of course, if you were to expand the range, the entire thing would fall over.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=170)** So, I probably wouldn't recommend putting anything to the right of a dynamic function.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/introduction-to-lambda-functions-with-bycol-and-byrow?u=76281980&t=177)** Unless of course, you are sure that this range is not going to expand, I'd probably just stick to using the BYCOL function.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (6), lambda (5), let (1)
> **Env Vars:** bycol (5), sum (4), byrow (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=142)** Again, the days, we can use the day function for that.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=148)** Now the depreciable number of days, so the day that we purchased the asset was day 12, and we need to deduct that from the 31,
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=169)** which will give us 19, and then I can just change my formatting, so it's 19 days that we need to depreciate for.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=178)** The next thing we need to do is to calculate the full month of depreciation, and of course we can do that using the SLN function, assuming that it is indeed straight line depreciation.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=191)** The cost is $156,000, there is no salvage, so I'll put another comma in there, and the life is 8 years.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=202)** So a full year of depreciation is $19,500, so to convert that to monthly, I can just divide that by 12, which will give us a full month of depreciation.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=215)** So then we need to convert that to daily by dividing this number by the 31 days that there is in March.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=224)** So that will give us an amount of $52 a day.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=227)** We might want to put an extra decimal place in there because the formatting is simply rounding it, and we can then work out the first month of depreciation by multiplying it by the 19 days.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculate-pro-data-rental-costs-with-date-functions?u=76281980&t=241)** And so we can see that our first month's depreciation charge is $996.

#### Calculating the payback period
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculating-the-payback-period?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculating-the-payback-period?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you are evaluating a financial opportunity, there are three generally accepted measures of project evaluation, the NPV, the IRR, and the payback period.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculating-the-payback-period?u=76281980&t=13)** Now the NPV and IRR are fairly straightforward to calculate, as there are dedicated Excel functions, but there isn't one for payback, and so we need to figure out a way to calculate it manually.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/calculating-the-payback-period?u=76281980&t=28)** So what the payback is telling us is how long it takes before the cashflow becomes positive.
>

#### Using RATE or RRI for compound annual growth rate (CAGR)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A popular way to measure the return of an asset or an investment is to use the Compound Annual Growth Rate.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=8)** It looks at for the period of investment, what the annual rate of return is.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=14)** So, basically it's the rate of return that would be required for an investment to grow from its beginning balance to its ending balance.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=23)** So the example that we're going to have a look at today looks something like this.

>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=35)** So, to work out the compound annual growth rate, we simply take into account the starting value of $16.57 million, and the ending value, so it grows up to around $66 million.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=49)** And we take into account the number of years that it took to achieve that result.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=57)** And we can work this out using an ordinary formula.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=60)** So, we can take the last year value divided by the first year value to the power of 1 on years, and then deduct 1 from it.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=69)** So, that's one way of doing it.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=71)** Or there are a couple of Excel functions that we can use to calculate it.

>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=86)** So, let's have a look now at how to use each of these methods in Excel.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=93)** So just for interest, let's calculate the year on year growth.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=97)** And we can do this by picking up the later year, dividing it by the previous year and minus 1.

> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=105)** And copy that over so you can see how that changes.

>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-rate-or-rri-for-compound-annual-growth-rate-cagr?u=76281980&t=118)** So, we are going to calculate that and we'll find that the number is quite different.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=263)** So that is going to apply, no inflation for the first year, and it's going to add one lot of inflation, two lots of inflation, three lots of inflation, and so on as it goes down the row.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=274)** So then what we need to do is to apply our true-false logic to get this to spill over to the right hand side.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=282)** So let's go to the beginning of our formula here and we are going to apply that.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=288)** Year hash is greater than or equal to this year, hash multiplied by the SLM function.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=299)** And I'm just going to put some brackets around it there.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=303)** And that's going to spill it over to the right hand side so that we get that waterfall technique.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=309)** But we are not quite finished.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=310)** What we need to do now is to divide the first year by two because we are going to assume that if the asset was purchased in 2024, we don't have a full amount of depreciation for the first year.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=326)** We're going to assume that it was purchased part of the way through the year.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=330)** So if it was purchased in 2024, we need to divide it by two.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=333)** So let's go to the end of the formula and we're going to divide it by, if the date in the schedule is equal to the year
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=348)** hash, value of true will be two, otherwise one.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=352)** So that is going to divide it by two if it is the year of purchase or it's going to just simply divide it by one if it's not the year of purchase.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-dynamic-arrays-to-create-a-depreciation-waterfall?u=76281980&t=363)** So let's test that.

#### Using NPV to calculate a discounted cash flow (DCF)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Discounted cash flow or DCF is one of the most commonly used methods for finding the value of a company or another cash flowing asset.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=10)** So sometimes we use a DCF method when we're trying to decide the value of an asset.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=16)** For example, you might be considering purchasing a company or a large piece of equipment and want to know what the value of that asset is for you.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=27)** Most of the time though, we use it to value a company, and that's what we're going to do in our example today.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=34)** In order to use the DCF method to arrive at a value for the asset or the company, you need the following three pieces of information.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=43)** You need to know the free cash flow to the firm or the information necessary to calculate this, you need the weighted average cost of capital, so we know how to calculate this because we did it in the previous movie, and you'll also need to make an assumption about the perpetuity growth rate.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=62)** So we usually come up with something like 2% for how much our cash flow is going to increase in perpetuity.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=71)** So in order to value a company, we would assume that the free cash flow will be received forever and ever out into the future.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=79)** So once our cash flows become stable, usually after about seven years, we can then calculate our terminal value, and we use a formula, we use this formula to calculate that.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=94)** So we're going to jump back into Excel now and do a very simple example that comes from Chapter 11 of my book, the second edition of "Financial Modeling in Excel for Dummies."
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=107)** So first of all, we'll calculate the WACC.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=109)** So based on what we did previously, this is a fairly simple way of doing it.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=113)** So we have got the cost of debt and we've got an assumption for the cost of equity, and we've just done this as a percentage of capital structure.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=123)** So we'll just multiply the cost of capital there and then add that to the equity, cost of debt multiply it by the proportion of equity, which gives us a weighted average cost of capital of 10.2%.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=138)** So we'll use that in our calculations.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=141)** So we've made an assumption that our cashflow is going to continue to grow by 2% into perpetuity.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=151)** And down here, we've got the free cash flow to the firm.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=154)** So these are just inputs based on our financial model calculations.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=160)** And down here, we need to work out the terminal value.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=164)** And we can do that using this formula here.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=168)** So we're going to work this out in Excel, so we are going to say that we start with the free cash flow, so that is the final year free cash flow, and we'll multiply it by 1 plus the growth rate, and then we'll divide it by the WACC minus the growth.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=200)** And we just need to put some brackets around there.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=207)** So that will give us the total cash flows that we can use in our DCF calculation.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=215)** So in seven years time, the terminal value of this cashflow in perpetuity is $264,000, so we add those together.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=225)** So these numbers we need to discount back into today's dollars.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=231)** So we'll go in and use our NPV function to do that.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=236)** Now our rate is 10.2%, our WACC number, and these are our cash flows.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=247)** So that's giving us a value of 215,460.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=251)** We need to add in the cash that we have, we need to deduct the debt, which is giving us an equity value of 192,960.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=263)** And if we wanted to work out the fair value per share, we could just divide it by the number of shares.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-financial-functions-in-depth-27987886/using-npv-to-calculate-a-discounted-cash-flow-dcf?u=76281980&t=270)** So that is our discounted cash flow.
